  67. The OralBreeze line is an immense improvement over the WaterPik-type dental irrigator for several reasons: - no countertop unit to take up valuable space - no electricity needed, so you don't have to worry about finding a free outlet that is close to the sink, or rig up an extension cord - an infinite water supply; there is no tank, so you never need to pause and refill it - immediately adjustable water temperature (more than once I have had to dump out my old WaterPik tank because the water was too hot or too cold) - quieter operation because there is no motor - greener/smaller carbon footprint/more sustainable/less to ultimately end up in a landfill, since it basically just consists of a hose, a dental tip, and some metal clamps and washer, and as mentioned in another context above, it uses no electricity I bought the QuickBreeze variety, which clamps on and off the faucet to an aerator that screws in to replace the standard one on the bathroom sink, and basically functions normally when the QuickBreeze is not in use. There is also a model called the RediBreeze, which stays connected to the sink all the time but has a switch that allows you to shift between it and the faucet, and a shower model, which allows you to perform at least two of your hygiene routines at once. It was remarkably easy to install. A small wrench was required to remove the old aerator, possibly because it had been in place so long that it was partially corroded, but this one screwed into place easily by hand. Water pressure is adjustable by hand, just as temperature is, and I found it every bit as forceful -- or not, as is your wont -- as an adjustable WaterPik. The only improvement I could suggest would be to change the tip design mount so that it could take WaterPik tips, and vice versa, since they are more readily available, but I suppose there are proprietary reasons why that will never happen. (Oh, and kudos to the OralBreeze company, from whom I bought it through Amazon, for dispatching it so rapidly.)

Maybe I'll learn to appreciate the shorter length down the road, but that's why I can't give an otherwise good product a 5 star rating. I've read how some people want to add extra chemicals to the water they shoot in, and it is true that you can't do that with this tool, but that's not going to be important to a majority of users. Bottom line: I'm pleased with the QuickBreeze - it was the right choice over other irrigators - but it's not perfect.

Already I can feel my gums improving (tingling). I give it 4 Stars simply because I can think of a few enhancements that would make it \\"perfect.\\" As background, I'm in my mid 50's, and after the eyes (presbyopia) the teeth go next. At my last checkup, my dentist saw the beginning of gingivitis and recommended a [[ASIN:B000GLRREU Waterpik Ultra Dental Water Jet]]. I was about to buy one locally and then thought \\"Amazon!\\" Good thing I did. Not only was the price lower on Amazon (with free shipping) but I saw a lot of 1 star reviews too. Fortunately, as with Macy's in the movie \\"Miracle on 34th Street,\\" Amazon isn't afraid to recommend other products to customers. I found the QuickBreeze in the \\"also viewed\\" and \\"also bought\\" section of the Waterpik page. Never would have known about the QuickBreeze otherwise. Glad I found it. I probably should send these ideas privately to Mr. Spaulding, but I put them in the public domain so he can use them if he wants without legal hassles of royalties for ideas. (I'm guessing he's thought about them already.) First, a really small nit... I wish the hose was straight, as shown in the their photo. Mine is coiled, perhaps because it was wrapped for shipping in a small box. Maybe it's a \\"Mr. Monk\\" thing (the obsessive detective on TV), but I'm always kinking the coil during use and trying to unwind it - in use, spraying water around the sink. Maybe it will straighten out with time or with heat? A hose without memory would be nice, so that it can be rolled up for stowage / travel but straighten out for use. Second and more important, I think the unit should \\"regulate\\" itself somewhat so that youcan't over-pressure it. The instructions warn about using too much water pressure, but also comment that everyone's house water pressure is different. My dentist gave me instructions for the Waterpik which said never to go past the 2nd lowest pressure setting. So apparently too much water pressure is a bad thing. But how much pressure is too much? I dunno. But I know it's too easy to overpressure with the QuickBreeze. It doesn't take more than a few degrees of rotation of my faucet to get a strong jet. (No one else in the house, so I don't know what happens if someone suddenly turns off a shower while you're irrigating. Does the pressure shoot up?) I'm not a Fluid Engineer, but they made me take Fluid Dynamics in college. I wonder if there's an easy way to set \\"choke flow\\" so that one cannot get too much pressure from the tap to the tip? If nothing else, it seems the I.D. of the inlet in the QB adapter is a lot bigger than it has to be. A smaller hole would act as a restrictor of sorts to keep the pressure from getting crazy high, as is done with low flow shower heads. (I might make my own insert to try this myself.) Or maybe a turbulator to increase fluid drag if the pressure is too high? If OralBreeze wanted to make a Deluxe version, maybe a cute clear flow meter at the hose adapter, where a colored ball or disk, whistle-like, rotates / spins to give an indication of pressure / flow? Or a spring loaded ball for a true pressure indicator? I saw one user request pulsed flow. I don't know enough about dentistry to know if there's any benefit to pulsed flow. I wonder if pulsing flow was simply a manifestation of the original Waterpik mechanics, where Marketing turned piston pulses into a \\"feature?\\" Maybe it just feels \\"cool.\\" But if there is some benefit, again a whistle-like device with a rotating ball blocking / unblocking an orifice would give a pulsing effect if it's really necessary. Having said all this, the QuickBreeze is a good product. It's cheaper than a Waterpik and less trouble. Nothing to leak and no reservoir to fuss with, grow bacteria in. When Oral Breeze comes up with a rubber based \\"Ty-wrap\\" temporary travel adapter that easily straps and unstraps around a hotel faucet aerator, it will be a lot easier to travel with than a Waterpik too.

There should be an optimal stream force, which would get rid of all the food particles and not cause damage to enamel or gums. Having the stream too strong will probably cause damage to the gums over time. In addition, brushing of the teeth or using this product causes bacteria to be released into the blood stream. Since most people brush their teeth everyday and don't get infections in their blood it's probably not a big deal, but if the stream is too strong and it's over done, maybe there is an extra risk? I don't know if anyone has studied that question since the design of this product doesn't currently allow precise setting of the force of the stream.   327. Brilliant. This simple, well-made product invites you to use it. Looking something like a stethoscope, with a flexible tube about 18 inches long, and packaged in a deceptively small box, the QuickBreeze irrigator comes as a welcome replacement for my long-in-the-tooth electric other-name-brand model. With no plug to worry about, no mouldy water tank to clean, and no noisy pulsating motor, this gadget is a pleasure to use. It comes with a plastic cleaning tip (the bit that goes in your mouth), and a spare of a different colour. With a twist, this locks into the working end of the tube. The chrome metal stethoscope end clamps to the outlet on the tap. You control the amount of flow (and pressure) and temperature by adjusting the tap handles. The instruction leaflet is brief, but the installation is rather intuitive, anyway. An adaptor for the faucet is included. I did have to fiddle with this for about five minutes, trying different combinations of washers (included) to prevent the water from spurting around the connection. The stethoscope end attaches to the adaptor with a push-down ring assembly, in which is a sealing washer and ballbearings, allowing the device to pivot around the faucet head. This does not make for a perfect seal, and some back-flow escapes around the swivel head, though not enough to matter. Be aware that there's a lot of pressure concentrated at the cleaning tip, so you should adjust the flow and temperature gradually. Think of this as a pressure washer for your mouth. The item is, perhaps, a little on the dear side, but considering the cost of dental work, it's still a bargain. And the hose could do with perhaps an extra 3 to 6 inches length. Other than those niggles, I'd say this is a valuable addition to your armoury of dental care. The fit and finish of the product is high and it's a breeze to attach and unhook. Put it somewhere to dry in between uses. I wish I'd discovered this product sooner.

The only moving part is the ring that clips it to the faucet. It can't change the water flow and puts out a **lot** of water in a continuous stream. If I run the water with the QuickBreeze attached but not in my mouth, water sprays all over the place.<br /><br />Though they say it fits most bathroom faucets, it didn't fit mine. But when I called them, QuickBreeze immediately sent me a set of adapter rings that worked fine. When I took it on a trip, the bathroom faucet in the hotel had a **rectangular** opening and didn't fit. The replacement aerator that comes with the QuickBreeze is **not** flow restricted and puts out lots more water than the aerator that came with the faucet.<br /><br />Using the QuickBreeze is really fast: 1) Run the the hot and cold water until the temperature is stable. 2) Turn off the water, clip on the QuickBreeze. 3) Put the tip in your mouth, turn on the water, and go to it. 4) Turn off the water, unclip the QuickBreeze, and you're done. Finally, I hang it up on the towel rack so it drains and won't grow mold. (The adapter head is so heavy it took a while to figure out how to keep it from falling on the floor.) The whole thing takes me about 50 seconds, much less time than a WaterPik.<br /><br />There's one area in my lower middle gum where if I get the QuickBreeze stream anywhere near it, it digs a hole in the gum that's painful for over an hour. That's silly: like a WaterPik, it does a terrific job of cleaning out food particles and massaging the gums, but it can't remove sticky dental plaque the way floss can.<br /><br />Pros:<br />Very fast to use<br />Does a good job cleaning out food particles<br />Hopefully will last longer than a WaterPik<br /><br />Cons:<br />Digs a hole in one area of my gum<br />Probably not a good travel solution<br />Easy to spray water all over if you're not carefullast longer than a WaterPik<br /><br />Cons:<br />Digs a hole in one area of my gum<br />Probably not a good travel solution<br />Easy to spray water all over if you're not careful

  500. This is an exellant product. I just got mine, but I already love it. It installed in about five minutes, and that's including time spent reading the instructions. To be honest, the instructions were slightly confusing. They talk about inner and outer threads, and I got confused about whether they were talking about the threads on the faucet or those on the aerator included with the QuickBreeze; because the aerator has both inner and outer threads to make it compatible with several types of faucets. But the instructions aren't bad, and if you're even a little bit handy, you really don't need to read them at all. It's obvious how this thing should be installed. You just unscrew the old aerator, and screw on the new one. Just make sure you remove all the old rubber washers and such along with your old aerator, as they may tend to stick to the faucet, and will prevent the new aerator from fitting properly. Once the new aerator is installed, it's very easy to pop the hose on and off, and I did not experience any leaking at all.<br /><br />Prior to purchasing my QuickBreeze, I used a [[ASIN:B00000J9DU Conair WJ7BR Interplak Water Jet]]. I like the QuickBreeze much better for several reasons.<br /> 1) the Interpak has an AC powered pump motor that is very loud. Seriously, it would wake up people sleeping in the next room. By contrast, the QuickBreeze is whisper quiet; no louder than my faucet is without it.<br /> 2) The Interplak's pump had to be primed whenever I used it to get the water to start flowing through the tubing. This didn't always work very well, and was a pain. There is no such problem with the QuickBreeze.<br /> 3) The Interplak would occasionally run out of water in its tank before I was done using it. So I would have to refill the tank, and sometimes re-prime the pump to get it going again. Again, there is no such problem with the QuickBreeze, because it's connected directly to the faucet, so its water supply is unlimited.<br /> 4) The QuickBreeze allowes me to use much higher water pressure than did the Interplak. I find this very helpful.<br /><br />There was one thing I did like better about the Interplak though: It had a button valve on the handle that let you stop the flow of water while it was depressed. This made it easier to put the nozzle in and out of my mouth without spraying myself or my surroundings. The QuickBreeze doesn't have any such valve. You can of course just turn off the faucet before you put it in your mouth, but then you have to adjust the water temperature and pressure while it's already in your mouth. I wish that the QuickBreeze had a button like the Interplak, but for all its other benefits, it is definitely worth the trade-off. For those who are interested, OralBreeze does make another similar product which does have a valve: The [[ASIN:B0028OV2S0 RediBreeze Oral Irrigator dental water jet]]. However, the RediBreeze has to be permanently installed, and does not pop on and off like the QuickBreeze does. Another trade-off, so pick what's more important to you.<br /><br />------------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />Update: All the praise about this company's awesome customer service is well deserved. After using my QuickBreeze for about a year, the screw-on fitting on the nozzle end of the hose split, and the hose popped off. I e-mailed OralBreeze about this, and the very next day they shipped me a new one, as well as a new set of tips. There was no hassle, no long process, no questions asked. They just took care of it. Service like this is a rare find!

Unless there is a reason you don't want a large permanent attachment on your faucet, I recommend getting RediBreeze, which I bought for my mother.<br />- If the QuickBreeze attachment is difficult to get on/off the faucet, then my suggestion is to apply silicone spray or vegetable oil to the inside of the attachment. I don't believe WD-40 is a good idea as it's not made for rubber and probably destroys it.<br />- when I bought the product, I had a choice of a long hose or short hose. If that choice is still available, chose the short hose as you'll be bending over the sink, and a long hose just gets in the way.
